[1mุงููุฑุงุฌุน ููุฑุงุกุงุช ุฅุถุงููุฉ [0m

[1mููุฏูุฉ [0m

David Reynoldsโs observation on the origins of diplomacy is in [3mSummits: Six Meetings That Shaped the Twentieth Century [0m(Basic 
Books, 2007). The Toynbee and Kissinger quotes are found in Daniel M. Smith and Joseph M. Siracusa, [3mThe Testing of America, 
1914โ1945 [0m(Forum Press, 1979) and Henry Kissinger, [3mDiplomacy [0m(Simon and Schuster, 1994), respectively. 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุฃูู [0m

To begin at the beginning, see Harold Nicolsonโs fadedโand fadingโclassic, [3mDiplomacy [0m(Harcourt Brace, 1939), which is not to be 
confused with Henry Kissingerโs otherwise brilliant diplomatic history, [3mDiplomacy [0m(Simon and Schuster, 1994), which, Kissinger 
assures us, is quite different in scope, intentions, and ideas. Indispensable is M. S. Andersonโs treatise on the evolution of 
diplomacy to 1919, [3mThe Rise of Modern Diplomacy, 1450โ1919 [0m(Longman, 1993). The history of diplomacy is admirably covered in G. R. 
Berridge, [3mDiplomacy: Theory and Practice, [0m4th edn. (Palgrave Macmillan, 2009); Keith Hamilton and Richard Langhorne, [3mThe Practice 
of Diplomacy: Its Evolution, Theory and Administration, [0m2nd edn. (Routledge, 2010); and Adam Watson, [3mDiplomacy: Dialogue between 
States [0m(Routledge, 1982). For a critique of the culture of traditional diplomatic services, see Shaun Riordan, [3mThe New Diplomacy [0m
(Polity Press, 2002). 

How diplomats represent state institutions in a complex relationship of facts designed to bring order to international society is 
explored in [3mThe Diplomatic Corps as an Institution of International Society [0m, ed. Paul Sharp and Geoffrey Wiseman (Palgrave 
Macmillan, 2008). G. R. Berridge has much to say in [3mReturn to the United Nations: UN Diplomacy in Regional Conflicts [0m(Palgrave 
Macmillan, 1991). For the emerging diplomacy of civil society, see Paul Battersby and Joseph M. Siracusa, [3mGlobalization and Human 
Security [0m(Rowman and Littlefield, 2009). Quotes on public diplomacy are taken from Charles Wolf, Jr, and Brian Rosen, [3mPublic 
Diplomacy: How To Think About It and Improve It [0m(RAND, 2004). Also useful is Walter R. Roberts, โThe Evolution of Diplomacyโ, [3m
Mediterranean Quarterly, [0m17 (Summer 2006): 55. 

For the history and significance of treaties, see Charles L. Philips and Alan Axelrod (eds.), [3mEncyclopedia of Historical Treaties 
and Alliances, [0m2 vols (Facts on File, 2001); J. A. S. Grenville, [3mThe Major International Treaties, 1914โ1973: A History and Guide 
with Texts [0m(Methuen, 1974); Mario Toscano, [3mThe History of Treaties and International Politics [0m(Johns Hopkins University Press, 
1966); and Eileen Denza, [3mDiplomatic Law, [0m3rd edn. (Oxford University Press, 2008). 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุซุงูู [0m

The best summary of the diplomacy of the American Revolution is Samuel Flagg Bemis, [3mThe Diplomacy of the American Revolution [0m(D. 
Appleton-Century, 1935). Bemis skilfully exploits archives in offering a Whig interpretation of an innocent America dealing with 
corrupt Europe. This interpretation has been challenged, but not the coverage and detailed analysis. Also useful are Jonathan R. 
Dull, [3mA Diplomatic History of the American Revolution [0m(Yale University Press, 1985); Reginald Horsman, [3mThe Diplomacy of the New 
Republic [0m(Harlan Davidson, 1985); and Robert R. Palmer, [3mThe Age of the Democratic Revolution: A Political History of Europe and 
America, 1760โ1800, [0mVol. 1: [3mThe Challenge [0m(Princeton University Press, 1959). 

Arthur M. Schlesingerโs [3mThe Colonial Merchants and the American Revolution, 1763โ1776 [0m(Longmans, 1918) is a landmark study of the 
origins of the American Revolution, showing the role merchants played in staving off radical measures of Parliament and colonials 
until 1776. 

The best general account of British ministerial politics and the American question for 1773 to 1775 is Bernard Donoughue, [3mBritish 
Politics and the American Revolution, the Path to War, 1773โ75 [0m(Macmillan, 1964). For a description of British military objectives 
and successes, see Piers Mackesy, [3mThe War for America, 1775โ1783 [0m(University of Nebraska Press, 1993). William C. Stinchcombe, [3mThe 
American Revolution and the French Alliance [0m(Syracuse University Press, 1969) analyses the domestic reaction to the French 
alliance in America, making the case that colonial Americans suspended their traditional anti-French and anti-Catholic beliefs to 
make it a success. For a discussion of domestic and international factors and influences, consult Richard W. Van Alstyne, [3mEmpire 
and Independence: The International History of the American Revolution [0m(John Wiley, 1965). 

Benjamin Franklin was the most important diplomat of the American Revolution and because of this has attracted much scholarly 
attention. The best studies of Franklin and his times are Claude A. Lopez and Eugenia W. Herbert, [3mThe Private Franklin: The Man 
and His Family [0m(Norton, 1975); Gerald Stourzh, [3mBenjamin Franklin and American Foreign Policy [0m(University of Chicago Press, 1954); 
and Carl Van Doren, [3mBenjamin Franklin [0m(Viking, 1938). 

The standard account of Henry Laurens, John Adams, Benjamin Franklin, and particularly John Jay, who negotiated the peace with 
Great Britain in 1782, is Richard B. Morris, [3mThe Peacemakers: The Great Powers and American Independence [0m(Harper and Row, 1965). 
This well-researched work is marred only by the authorโs excessive distrust of Vergennes and Europeans in general. Valuable 
insights are found in Lawrence S. Kaplan, โThe Treaty of Paris 1783: A Historiographical Challengeโ, [3mInternational History Review, [0m
5 (August 1983): 431โ42; and Ronald Hoffman and Peter J. Albert (eds.), [3mPeace and Peacemakers: The Treaty of 1783 [0m(University of 
Virginia Press for the United States Capitol Historical Society, 1986). The Royal Instructions to the Peace Commission of 1778 are 
conveniently located in S. E. Morison (ed.), [3mSources and Documents Illustrating the American Revolution, 1764โ1788 [0m(The Clarendon 
Press, 1923). 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุซุงูุซ [0m

The best general introductions to European history covered by this chapter are Norman Rich, [3mGreat Power Diplomacy, 1814โ1914 [0m
(McGraw-Hill, 1992); A. J. P. Taylor, [3mThe Struggle for Mastery in Europe, 1848โ1918 [0m(Oxford University Press, 1954); Christopher 
J. Bartlett, [3mThe Global Conflict: The International Rivalry of the Great Powers, 1880โ1990 [0m(Longman, 1994); Norman Stone, [3mEurope 
Transformed, 1878โ1919 [0m(Oxford University Press, 1999); and James Joll, [3mEurope since 1870, [0m4th edn. (Penguin, 1990). 
Nineteenth-century diplomacy is treated in Christopher J. Bartlett, [3mPeace, War and the European Powers, 1814โ1914 [0m(Palgrave 
Macmillan, 1996); and F. R. Bridge and Roger Bullen, [3mThe Great Powers and the European States System, 1815โ1914 [0m(Longman, 1980). 
Useful access to primary source material is provided by Ralph R. Menning (ed.), [3mThe Art of the Possible: Documents on Great Power 
Diplomacy, 1814โ1914 [0m(McGraw-Hill, 1996). 

For the breakdown of Bismarckโs alliance system, see Richard Langhorne, [3mThe Collapse of the Concert of Europe: International 
Politics, 1890โ1914 [0m(Palgrave Macmillan, 1981); William L. Langer, [3mThe Franco-Russian Alliance, 1890โ1894 [0m(Harvard University 
Press, 1929) and [3mThe Diplomacy of Imperialism [0m, 2nd edn. (Knopf, 1968); and George F. Kennan, [3mThe Decline of Bismarckโs European 
Order: Franco-Russian Relations, 1875โ1890 [0m(Princeton University Press, 1979). James Joll and Gordon Martelโs [3mThe Origins of the 
First World War [0m, 3rd edn. (Oxford University Press, 2006) remains the best general introduction to the subject, while the best 
military history of World War I is B. H. Liddell Hart, [3mHistory of the First World War [0m(Weidenfeld and Nicolson, 1970). For the 
harm done on the ground level, see Alan Kramer, [3mDynamic of Destruction: Culture and Mass Killing in the First World War [0m(Oxford 
University Press, 2007); and Alexander Watson, [3mEnduring the Great War: Combat, Morale and Collapse in the German and British 
Armies, 1914โ1918 [0m(Cambridge University Press, 2008). 

For the entry of the United States into the war and its subsequent rejection of the Treaty of Versailles, see Thomas J. Knock, [3mTo 
End All Wars: Woodrow Wilson and the Quest for a New World Order [0m(Princeton University Press, 1995); Daniel M. Smith, [3mThe Great 
Departure: United States and World War I, 1914โ1920 [0m(Wiley, 1965); and Arthur S. Link, [3mWilson, the Diplomatist [0m(Johns Hopkins 
University Press, 1957). 

For Germanyโs โSeptember Programโ, see Fritz Fischer, [3mGermanyโs War Aims in the First World War [0m(Norton, 1967). Fischer lays the 
blame for war squarely on Berlin. John A. Mosesโs [3mThe Politics of Illusion: The Fischer Controversy in German Historiography [0m
(Barnes and Noble, 1975) presents a detailed review of Fischerโs revisionist thesis. Jay Winter and Antoine Prostโs [3mThe Great War 
in History: Debates and Controversies, 1914 to the Present [0m(Cambridge University Press, 2006) is an important comparative study, 
analysing a multitude of books on World War I written by French, British, and German scholars in order to show patterns of themes 
and methods over time. 

For the historical debate surrounding the course and consequences of the Treaty of Versailles, see Manfred F. Boemeke, Gerald D. 
Feldman, and Elisabeth Glaser (eds.), [3mThe Treaty of Versailles: A Reassessment after 75 Years [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1998). 
Quotes by David Lloyd George, Winston Churchill, and John Maynard Keynes in this chapter are found in David Lloyd George, [3mBritish 
War Aims [0m(George H. Doran, 1917) and [3mWar Memoirs [0m(Little Brown, 1932โ7); Winston Churchill, [3mThe World Crisis [0m(Butterworth, 
1923โ31), vol. 5; and John Maynard Keynes, [3mThe Economic Consequences of the Peace [0m(Harcourt, Brace and Howe, 1920), respectively. 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุฑุงุจุน [0m

Before all else, I should like to pay tribute to John Lukacs, whose โThe Night Stalin and Churchill Divided Europeโ, [3mThe New York 
Times Magazine, [0m5 October 1969, 37โ50, inspired a generation of scholarship, including my own. See Joseph M. Siracusa, [3mInto the 
Dark House: American Diplomacy and the Ideological Origins of the Cold War [0m(Regina Books, 1998) and โThe Meaning of TOLSTOY: 
Churchill, Stalin, and the Balkans, Moscow, October 1944โ, [3mDiplomatic History, [0m3 (Fall 1979), 443โ63, which is a discussion of the 
source material for this meeting located in the Public Record Office. Also useful are Albert Resis, โThe Churchill-Stalin Secret 
โPercentagesโ Agreement on the Balkans, Moscow, October 1944โ, [3mAmerican Historical Review, [0m85 (1981), 368โ87, and โSpheres of 
Influence in Soviet Diplomacyโ, [3mJournal of Modern History, [0m53 (1981), 417โ39; and Vojtech Mastny, [3mRussiaโs Road to the Cold War: 
Diplomacy, Warfare, and the Politics of Communism, 1941โ1945 [0m(Columbia University Press, 1979). 

Recommended overviews of the war years include the first volume in Norman A. Graebner, Richard Dean Burns, and Joseph M. Siracusa, [3m
America and the Cold War, 1941โ1991: A Realist Interpretation, [0m2 vols (Praeger Security International, 2010); Gerhard L. Weinberg, [3m
A World at Arms: A Global History of World War II [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1994); William H. McNeill, [3mAmerica, Britain and 
Russia: Their Cooperation and Conflict, 1941โ1946 [0m(Oxford University Press, 1953); Herbert Feis, [3mChurchill, Roosevelt, Stalin: The 
War They Waged and the Peace They Sought [0m(Princeton University Press, 1957); and John L. Snell, [3mIllusion and Necessity: The 
Diplomacy of Global War, 1939โ1945 [0m(Houghton Mifflin, 1963). On the British side, see Sir Lleywellyn Woodard, [3mHistory of the 
Second World War: British Foreign Policy in the Second World War, [0m5 vols (HMSO, 1970โ6) and John Charmley, [3mChurchillโs Grand 
Alliance: The Anglo-American Special Relationship, 1940โ57 [0m(Hodder and Stoughton, 1995). The best reference work for the period is [3m
The Oxford Companion to the Second World War [0m, ed. I. C. B. Dear (Oxford University Press, 1995). 

Winston Churchillโs recollection of events is found in Chapter 15, โOctober in Moscowโ, in [3mThe Second World War [0m, vol. 6, [3mTriumph 
and Tragedy [0m(Houghton Mifflin, 1953). Also useful are [3mThe Diaries of Sir Alexander Cadogan, 1939โ45 [0m, ed. David Dilks (Cassell, 
1971); Anthony Eden, [3mThe Memoirs of Anthony Eden, Earl of Avon: The Reckoning [0m(Houghton Mifflin, 1965); W. Averell Harriman and 
Elie Abel, [3mSpecial Envoy to Churchill and Stalin, 1941โ1946 [0m(Random House, 1975); R. E. Sherwood, [3mRoosevelt and Hopkins: An 
Intimate History [0m(Putnamโs, 1977); Charles E. Bohlen, [3mWitness to History, 1929โ1969 [0m(Norton, 1973); and George F. Kennan, [3mMemoirs, 
1925โ1963 [0m(Little, Brown, 1967). 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุฎุงูุณ [0m

That Australian scholars have been talking and writing about the United States far more than American scholars have been talking 
and writing about Australia should come as no surprise. See Joseph M. Siracusa, โThe United Sates, Australia, and the Central 
Pacificโ, in [3mGuide to American Foreign Relations since 1700 [0m, ed. Richard Dean Burns (ABC-CLIO, 1983). Also see Joseph M. Siracusa 
and Yeong-Han Cheong, [3mAmericaโs Australia/Australiaโs America: A Guide to Issues and References [0m(Regina Books, 1997); and Joseph 
M. Siracusa and David G. Coleman, [3mAustralia Looks to America: Australian-American Relations since Pearl Harbor [0m(Regina Books, 
2006). 

For general background, see C. Hartley Grattan, [3mThe United States and the Southwest Pacific [0m(Harvard University Press, 1961); 
Warner Levi, [3mAmerican-Australian Relations [0m(University of Minnesota Press, 1947); and Gordon Greenwood, [3mEarly Australian-American 
Relations [0m(Melbourne University Press, 1944). 

Trevor R. Reeseโs [3mAustralia, New Zealand, and the United States [0m(Oxford University Press, 1969) provides a broad survey of the 
conclusion and operation of the ANZUS Treaty, while Joseph G. Starkeโs [3mANZUS Treaty Alliance [0m(Melbourne University Press, 1965) 
remains the classic treatment of the subject. 

Also useful are Harry C. Gelber, [3mThe Australian-American Alliance: Costs and Benefits [0m(Penguin, 1968); Henry S. Albinski, [3mANZUS, 
the United States and Pacific Security [0m(University Press of America, 1987); and Coral Bell, [3mDependent Ally: A Study in Australian 
Foreign Policy [0m(Oxford University Press, 1988). 

Memoir literature includes D. Dilks (ed.), [3mThe Diaries of Sir Alexander Cadogan, 1938โ45 [0m(Cassell, 1971); Walter Millis (ed.), [3mThe 
Forrestal Diaries: The Inner History of the Cold War [0m(Cassell, 1952); and P. C. Spender, [3mExercises in Diplomacy: The ANZUS Treaty 
and the Colombo Plan [0m(Sydney University Press, 1969). Primary documents for ANZUS may be found in [3mThe ANZUS Documents [0m, ed. A. 
Burnett (Australian National University, 1991) and [3mAustralian-American Relations since 1945 [0m, ed. Glen St J. Barclay and Joseph M. 
Siracusa (Holt, Rinehart and Winston, 1976). 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุณุงุฏุณ [0m

Portions of this chapter have been adapted from my recent study, with Paul Battersby, [3mGlobalization and Human Security [0m(Rowman and 
Littlefield, 2009). 

The concept of globalization has gathered a rich bibliography in a relatively short period of time and become embedded in the 
social science lexicon. A good general and brief study of globalization is Manfred Stegerโs [3mGlobalization: A Very Short 
Introduction [0m(Oxford University Press, 2003). For a comprehensive and multi-layered introduction, see John Baylis and Steve Smith 
(eds.), [3mThe Globalization of World Politics: An Introduction to International Relations [0m(Oxford University Press, 2001). Baylis 
and Smith bring together authored chapters on history, political theory, conflict and security, international institutions, 
environmental politics, and human rights. 

For the economic dimensions of globalization, see Jurgen Osterhammel, Niels Petersen, and Donna Geyer, [3mGlobalization: A Short 
History [0m(Princeton University Press, 2005); Niall Ferguson, [3mThe Cash Nexus: Money and Power in the Modern World [0m(Basic Books, 
2001); and Erich Rauchway, [3mBlessed among Nations: How the World Made America [0m(Hill and Wang, 2006). 

The processes of what we recognize as globalization span several centuries. For different historical perspectives, see David Held, 
Anthony McGrew, David Goldblatt, and Jonathan Perraton, [3mGlobal Transformations: Politics, Economics, and Culture [0m(Stanford 
University Press, 1999). Also useful are Anthony Giddens, [3mThe Consequences of Modernity [0m(Polity Press, 1990) and [3mRunaway World [0m
(Routledge, 2000); Frederic Jameson and Massao Mioyshi (eds.), [3mThe Cultures of Globalization [0m(Duke University Press, 1998); and 
James H. Mittleman (ed.), [3mThe Globalization Syndrome [0m(Princeton University Press, 2000). 

For critical perspectives on human security, see Giorgio Shani, Makoto Sato, and Mustapha Kamal Pasha (eds.), [3mProtecting Human 
Security in a Post 9/11 World: Critical and Global Insights [0m(Palgrave Macmillan, 2007). Also see International Commission on 
Intervention and State Sovereignty, [3mThe Responsibility to Protect [0m(International Development Research Center, 2001); and Andrew 
Mack, [3mThe Human Security Report 2005: War and Peace in the 21st Century [0m(Oxford University Press, 2005). 

The quotes in this chapter may be found in Nicholas Stern, [3mThe Stern Review: The Economics of Climate Change [0m(HM Treasury, 2006); 
Roland Paris, โHuman Security: Paradigm Shift or Hot Air?โ, in [3mNew Global Dangers: Changing Dimensions of International Security [0m, 
ed. M. E. Brown, O. R. Cote, Jr, S. M. Lynn-Jones, and S. E. Miller (MIT Press, 2004); Jim Whitman, [3mThe Limits of Global 
Governance [0m(Routledge, 2005); Manuel Mejido Costoya, [3mToward a Typology of Civil Actors: The Case of the Movement to Change 
International Trade Rules and Barriers [0m(United Nations Research Institute for Social Development, 2007); and Nina Graeger and 
Alexandra Novosseloff, โThe Role of the OSCE and the EUโ, in [3mThe United Nations and Regional Security: Europe and Beyond [0m, ed. 
Michael Pugh and Waheguru Pal Singh Sidhu (Lynne Rienner, 2003).
